Wilson v. State


A04-575


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1233



November 9, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Wilson v. State, 2005 Minn. LEXIS 35 (2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Hennepin County; Hon. David Duffy, Judge. District Court File No. 99005185. [*2] 
Wilson v. State, 2003 Minn. App. LEXIS 83 (Minn. Ct. App., Jan. 28, 2003)



DISPOSITION: District court's denial of petition for post-conviction relief affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate appealed a decision of the Hennepin County District Court (Minnesota), which denied his second petition of postconviction relief. The inmate was convicted of second-degree felony murder and second-degree murder of an unborn child.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was represented by counsel in his first postconviction petition and on appeal from the denial of it. In the second petition, the inmate argued that Minnesota murder-of-an-unborn-child statute, Minn. Stat. § 609.2662(2) (2002), was unconstitutional and violated the Fifth Amendment's guarantee of due process as applied to the states through the Fourteenth Amendment. The district court concluded that the claims were procedurally barred, because the inmate did not raise them in his first petition, nor did any exceptions apply. The court affirmed and held that the inmate's constitutional arguments were based on principles of law that had not been altered in the two years since his first petition. Thus, the inmate's claims were all known, but not raised at the time of his first postconviction appeal and were therefore barred.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of postconviction relief.

Bandy v. State


A04-850


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1244



November 2, 2004, Decided  
November 9, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Bandy v. State, 2005 Minn. LEXIS 109 (2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Hennepin County; Hon. Lucy Wieland, Judge. District Court File No. 98108691.
State v. Bandy, 2000 Minn. App. LEXIS 504 (Minn. Ct. App., May 23, 2000)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's convictions for criminal sexual conduct and depriving parental rights were affirmed on appeal. The Hennepin County District Court, Minnesota, denied defendant's petition for postconviction relief, and his appeal from denial of that petition was dismissed on procedural grounds. Defendant's subsequent motion to amend and correct his sentence was treated as a petition for postconviction relief, which was also denied. Defendant appealed.

The issues of his alleged excessive sentence were improperly raised in his reply brief; as such, they were not properly before the instant court.

483 of 1980 DOCUMENTS


Williams v. Dakota County Cmty. Dev. Agency


A04-6


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1199



October 19, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


PRIOR HISTORY: Dakota County Community Development Authority.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a certiorari appeal, relator, a recipient of housing benefits (the recipient), argued that respondent, the Dakota County Community Development Agency (CDA), Minnesota, lacked legal authority to terminate her housing benefits for failing to report a change in the size of her household. The CDA asserted that the recipient failed to report that an additional person, the father of one of her children, moved into her household unit.


OVERVIEW: Under the applicable regulations, the recipient was required to report to the CDA that any other persons had become members of the household. It was also found that the subject additional person would not have been allowed to reside in the household, because he had a felony on his record. Under Section 8 Tenant Based Assistance, 24 C.F.R. § 982.552(c)(i) (2004), the CDA could terminate the assistance of a program participant, if the family violated any family obligations under the program, and both of the latter matters constituted violations of the applicable regulations. The appellate court rejected the recipient's argument that any violation in failing to provide accurate information to the CDA was not serious enough to warrant termination of rent assistance, and found that, based on the record of the agency proceeding, particularly the testimony of the recipient, the additional person, and two related family members, the CDAs decision was not an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The decision of the county community development authority was affirmed.

Thorson v. Billy Graham Evangelistic Ass'n


A04-404


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


687 N.W.2d 652; 2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1205; 94 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1148



October 19, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Sara Thorson v. Billy Graham Evangelistc Ass'n, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 789 (Minn., Dec. 22, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Hennepin County District Court. File No. 03-12062.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, former employee, challenged the judgment of the Hennepin County District Court, Minnesota, granting summary judgment in favor of respondent, religious association, dismissing her employment discrimination action under the Minnesota Human Rights Act (MHRA), alleging that the association terminated her employment on the basis of her sexual orientation.


OVERVIEW: The association promoted Christianity through a combination of live events, productions for film and television, and publication of books, pamphlets, and magazines. Its employees were required to profess Christianity and participate in devotional activities. The employee worked in the mailroom. Two employees reported seeing the employee kissing another woman in the parking lot at work. The employee admitted that she was a lesbian. Based on a determination that the employee's sexual orientation was inconsistent with the association's mission, the association terminated the employee's employment. The employee argued that because she was engaged in a secular business activity as a mailroom employee, the association was not exempt from the operation of the MHRA. The appellate court ruled that because the association's business activities were exclusively related to its evangelical ministry, they were not secular business activities unrelated to the religious and educational purposes for which the association was organized. The association was exempt under Minn. Stat. § 363A.26(2) (Supp. 2003) from the sexual-orientation provisions of the MHRA.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Conley


A03-1669


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1160



October 12, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Conley, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 796 (2004)
Appeal after remand at, Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Conley, 2009 Minn. App. Unpub. LEXIS 154 (Minn. Ct. App., Feb. 3, 2009)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Conley v. Smith, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 147183 (D. Minn., Sept. 10, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY: Ramsey County; Hon. Joanne M. Smith, Judge. District Court File No. K8-03-1055.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from his conviction and sentence entered by the Ramsey County District Court, Minnesota, for first-degree criminal sexual conduct, soliciting to practice prostitution and second-degree assault.


OVERVIEW: Defendant lived with the victim and her two daughters in the victim's apartment. The victim testified that defendant attempted to force her into prostitution, threatened to kill her and her family members, held her hostage, and abused her repeatedly. Defendant argued that the district court erred in denying his motion to suppress evidence allegedly obtained through an illegal search. Defendant conceded that the victim had authority to consent to a search of the apartment, actually consented, and that the victim entered the apartment with the police after defendant had been arrested. The appellate court ruled that defendant's consent to the search was irrelevant, and the district court did not err in ruling that the evidence was admissible because it was obtained from a lawful search. Also, the appellate court determined that the upward durational departure sentence for first-degree criminal sexual conduct should be remanded. If a recent U.S. Supreme Court case applied to the Minnesota sentencing guidelines, defendant would be entitled to the benefit of the rule established in that case.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed, and the sentence for first-degree criminal sexual conduct was remanded.

Taylor v. State


A04-383


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1166



October 12, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Richard Wayne Taylor v. Minn., 2004 Minn. LEXIS 790 (Minn., Dec. 22, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Ramsey County; Hon. Joanne M. Smith, Judge. District Court File Nos. K0-02-2075, K4-01-1307.
Taylor v. State, 670 N.W.2d 584, 2003 Minn. LEXIS 716 (2003)
State v. Taylor, 2003 Minn. App. LEXIS 931 (Minn. Ct. App., Aug. 5, 2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate appealed a decision of the Ramsey County District Court (Minnesota), which denied postconviction relief.


OVERVIEW: The inmate claimed that his convictions were invalid because the statutes under which he was convicted, Minn. Stat. §§ 609.342, subd. 1(a) and 609.343, subd. 1(a) (2002), did not contain a title or enactment clause, in violation of the Minnesota Constitution. The court noted that the purpose of Minn. Const. art. IV, § 17 was to prevent fraud or surprise in the legislative process by ensuring that the public had notice of the bill's contents. The court held that the district court properly denied the inmate's claim for postconviction relief and that both Minn. Stat. §§ 609.342, subd. 1(a) and 609.343, subd. 1(a) (2002) had similar titles, and given that the titles provided a plain description of their respective acts, the titles provided reasonable notice of the acts' contents. The inmate also argued that the two provisions did not contain an enactment clause required by Minn. Const. art. IV, § 22. The court held that copies of Minnesota statutes did not need to contain the enactment clauses of the statutes contained therein.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of postconviction relief.

In re Civ. Commitment of: Hartleib


A04-863


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1174



October 12, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by In re Civ. Commitment Of: Lloyd Anthony Hartleib, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 824 (Minn., Dec. 14, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Anoka County; Hon. James A. Morrow, Judge. District Court File No. PX-99-80.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sex offender challenged a judgment of the Anoka County District Court (Minnesota), which ordered his indeterminate civil commitment to the Minnesota Sex Offender Program as a sexual psychopathic personality (SPP) and a sexually dangerous person (SDP).


OVERVIEW: The offender argued that his commitment without a jury trial violated his constitutional rights and that the provisions of the SPP and SDP statute, Minn. Stat. § 253B.18, subd. 1 (2002), were unconstitutional as applied to him. The court noted that the civil commitment act had withstood constitutional challenges identical to those made by the offender. Neither the state constitution nor federal due process required a jury trial before a person was committed as a SPP or SDP under state law. Furthermore, by failing to identify any constitutional defects in the statutory provisions or in their application to him, the offender had not met his burden of proof. The offender met the criteria for commitment as a SPP and a SDP, Minn. Stat. § 253B.02 (2002). Experts testified that their opinions were based on the use of actuarial tests, interviews, and a review of pertinent records and that the offender was only partially treated. They also testified that they had weighed the offender's amenability to continued treatment and his family support, but nevertheless concluded that he was utterly unable to control his sexual conduct and remained highly likely to re-offend sexually.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

State v. Hearn


C3-01-1318


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1176



October 12, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Hearn, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 799 (2004)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Hearn, 2006 Minn. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1221 (2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Hennepin County; Hon. Ann Leslie Alton, Judge. District Court File No. 00087369.
State v. Hearn, 647 N.W.2d 27, 2002 Minn. App. LEXIS 775 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Hennepin County District Court (Minnesota), contesting the validity of his conviction for kidnapping, in violation of Minn. Stat. § 609.25, subds. 1(2), 2(2) (1998). He had also been convicted of attempted first-degree criminal sexual conduct and second-degree criminal sexual conduct.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the kidnapping was merely incidental to the sexual assault and attempted sexual assault against the victim. Defendant confined or removed the victim twice, once inside a restroom stall and again when he forced her into his car. In affirming defendant's kidnapping conviction, the court held that, aside from the fact that a sexual assault could be committed in a space less confining than a restroom stall, defendant's conduct in pulling the victim back into the stall when she tried to escape went beyond the force or coercion required for criminal sexual conduct. Thus, the confinement in the stall was not merely incidental to commission of criminal sexual conduct. Defendant claimed that his upward sentencing departure for the kidnapping violated his Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial under the United States Supreme Court's Blakely decision. The court found that, even if it were to conclude that Blakely applied to the Minnesota sentencing guidelines, it would still be necessary to determine what was the appropriate remedy. The court stated that the trial court was in the best position to resolve those issues.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's kidnapping conviction, but remanded his kidnapping sentence for consideration of the possible application of Blakely to the sentence, and for determination of the appropriate remedy if Blakely applied.

Hy-Vee Food Stores v. Dep't of Health


A04-548


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1204



October 8, 2004, Decided  
October 19, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by Hy-Vee Food Stores, Inc. v. Minn. Dep't of Health, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 759 (2004)
Affirmed by Hy-Vee Food Stores, Inc. v. Minn. Dep't of Health, 705 N.W.2d 181, 2005 Minn. LEXIS 653 (Minn., Oct. 27, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Minnesota Department of Health. File No. 70900155012.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator, grocery store company, challenged the judgment of respondent, Minnesota Department of Health, imposing a three-year disqualification from participating in the Special Supplemental Nutrition Program for Women, Infants, and Children (WIC) for violating program rules prohibiting the sale of tobacco for a WIC voucher.


OVERVIEW: One of the company's stores allowed a package of cigarettes to be purchased with a WIC voucher. The Department found that the inclusion of the cigarettes on the voucher was entirely inadvertent, but disqualified the company from the WIC program for three years under state and federal regulations that mandated such disqualification for even one instance of the sale of tobacco products in exchange for a WIC voucher. The appellate court ruled that the evidence and the law supported the conclusion that a violation occurred, and the department's regulations did not arbitrarily and capriciously sanction unintentional rule violations. 7 C.F.R. § 246.12(l)(1)(iii)(A) required a state agency to disqualify a vendor for three years for one incidence of the sale of alcohol or tobacco products in exchange for a food instrument, thus the company's three-year disqualification was not unwarranted in law. Since the company conceded that it exchanged a pack of cigarettes for a WIC voucher, the disqualification was also not without justification in fact. The sanction, although harsh, was not arbitrary and capricious.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

490 of 1980 DOCUMENTS


State v. Birk


A03 -1903


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


687 N.W.2d 634; 2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1138



October 5, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Anoka County District Court. File No. K3-03-4705.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with third-degree driving under the influence of alcohol (DWI), Minn. Stat. §§ 169A.20, subd. 1(1) and 169A.27, subd. 1(a) (2002), and third-degree DWI, Minn. Stat. §§ 169.20, subd. 1(5) and 169A.27, subd. 1(a) (2002). The Anoka County District Court, Minnesota, found defendant guilty of the charged offenses, and defendant appeal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped for an improper lane change, and the officer noticed a strong odor of alcohol. The officer observed that defendant had slurred speech and bloodshot, watery eyes. The officer asked defendant to perform several field sobriety tests, and, based on defendant's performance, the officer believed that defendant was under the influence of alcohol and placed her under arrest for DWI. The officer administered the Intoxilyzer test, which produced an alcohol concentration reading of .14. The appellate court ruled that a jury could have reasonably found defendant guilty of testing .10 or more within two hours of driving and driving under the influence of alcohol. The officer testified that defendant gave two adequate breath samples and that the machine performed a calibration standard analysis between each sample. Also, the admission of the Intoxilyzer test, without antecedent expert testimony as to the test method, did not create an unconstitutional presumption of guilt, despite the jury instruction that the jury must evaluate both the reliability of the test method and the reliability of the test result.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Coopman


A04-236


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1141



October 5, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


PRIOR HISTORY: Blue Earth County; Hon. Norbert P. Smith, Judge. District Court File No. K1-03-1412.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Blue Earth County District Court, Minnesota, which convicted him of theft under Minn. Stat. § 609.52, subd. 2 (1) (2002).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a passenger in a car that was stopped for speeding. An officer knew defendant from prior contact and knew he had been present during the execution of a search warrant at an active methamphetamine lab two days prior to the stop. While the driver, who was suspected of being under the influence of a controlled substance, was being processed for the stop, a narcotics detection dog was brought to the scene. When the dog alerted to the presence of drugs, the passengers were removed from the car. Defendant was leasing the car, and signed a consent form to search the car. The officers searched the trunk and found stolen tools. The appellate court ruled that the district court did not err in denying defendant's motion to suppression. Because the driver was speeding, displayed behavior consistent with a person under the influence, and an officer recognized defendant as being involved with illegal narcotics activity, the police were within legal authority to seize the driver and conduct a dog sniff. The officers had probable cause to search the car, as the dog alerted to the presence of narcotics, and another passenger produced drugs and a pipe.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Coalition for Non-profit Student Housing v. City of Minneapolis


A03-1873


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1149



October 5, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


PRIOR HISTORY: Minneapolis City Council. Agency File No. 2003R-495.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent city designated certain fraternity and sorority houses, which appellant coalition owned, as historic. The coalition sought review.


OVERVIEW: On review, the coalition contended, inter alia, the city's designation of the chapter-house district was arbitrary and capricious because the parameters of the designation were not justified criteria, and the period of significance used in defining houses built as chapter houses was arbitrary. The appellate court found that the city based its decision on a 182-page report, transcripts from various meetings, supplemental reports, and presentations from the opposing sides. Thus, the parameters were not arbitrary and capricious because the city did not act on a whim and the record contained articulated reasons for the parameters of the historic designation and significant period. Further, the district was a defined area because of historical significance and was not an arbitrary designation simply because the properties were non-contiguous. The city's reasons for the historic designation had a factual basis that was supplied either by the initial report or the supplemental report, which was prepared following extensive questions and comments at the public hearing. Thus, similarly situated properties were not treated differently within the parameters of the study.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

493 of 1980 DOCUMENTS


Ledden v. State


A04-445


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


686 N.W.2d 873; 2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1109



September 28, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Howard Thomas Ledden v. Minn., 2004 Minn. LEXIS 792 (Minn., Dec. 22, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Ramsey County District Court. File No. K1-96-793.
State v. Ledden, 1998 Minn. App. LEXIS 206 (Minn. Ct. App., Feb. 24, 1998)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was found guilty of first- and second-degree criminal sexual conduct. On appeal, his conviction and sentence were affirmed. Defendant filed a subsequent motion for postconviction relief, alleging that the Ramsey County District Court (Minnesota) lacked jurisdiction because the laws as printed in the Minnesota statutes did not contain an enacting clause. The district court denied the motion. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the district court lacked subject-matter jurisdiction and that the statute under which he was convicted was void. The appellate court found no constitutional deficiency in defendant's convictions. Defendant argued that his convictions violated the state constitution and were therefore void. Defendant was convicted of first- and second-degree criminal sexual conduct in violation of Minn. Stat. §§ 609.342, subd. 1(a), .343, subd. 1(g) (1994). At the time of his conviction, both Minn. Stat. § 609.342 and Minn. Stat. § 609.343 were compiled from portions of 10 legislative enactments. Each of the acts creating and amending the statutes, as published in the Laws of Minnesota, began with the precise phrase, "Be it enacted by the legislature of the state of Minnesota." Therefore, defendant's conviction was not void because the statutes under which he was convicted complied with the constitutional mandates of Minn. Const. art. IV, § 22. His argument also confused the laws of Minnesota with the codified statutes. There was no constitutional deficiency in the district court's jurisdiction and the judge was legally qualified to rule on the merits of the instant case.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

State v. Petelshek


A04-584


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1113



September 28, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Minn. v. David Petelshek, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 832 (Minn., Dec. 14, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Ramsey County; Hon. Paulette K. Flynn, Judge. District Court File No. KX032322.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Ramsey County District Court, Minnesota, convicting him of fifth-degree controlled-substance crime.


OVERVIEW: The officer testified that he determined that he needed to stop defendant after seeing him and another man turn and walk away from one another immediately after seeing the squad car at the intersection. At that time, the officer knew that the intersection was the site of frequent drug activity and that defendant had a history of drug use. Defendant initially told the officer that he did not have any needles on him, but later admitted that he had a needle. The officer searched defendant and found a needle, a spoon with etch-marks from a needle, and a film canister that contained methamphetamine. The appellate court ruled that the officer who stopped defendant had a reasonable, articulable suspicion of criminal activity, and the district court did not err when it denied defendant's suppression motion. The officer knew defendant and that he had a history of drug use. The intersection was known for drug activity, and, after defendant observed the squad car, he turned and walked away. Also, defendant failed to show that he received ineffective assistance of counsel, because counsel's actions did not fall below an objective standard of reasonable representation.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

In the Matter of the Appeal of Rocheleau


A03-2046


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


686 N.W.2d 882; 2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1121



September 28, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by In re The Appeal of Robert, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 793 (Minn., Dec. 22, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Carver County Board of Commissioners.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator property owners challenged a decision of the Carver County Board of Commissioners (Minnesota), which affirmed respondent county environmental services' decision that their septic system had the potential to immediately threaten public health and requiring them to submit a new design for a replacement septic system under the county's individual-sewage-treatment system (ISTS) ordinance, Carver County, Minn., Code Ordinances § 52.002 (2001).


OVERVIEW: The owners argued that the ordinance was preempted by state law and that the statute violated the Due Process Clauses of the state and federal constitutions. In affirming, the court held that the doctrine of preemption did not preclude the county from regulating the owners' ISTS through its own ordinance because Minn. Stat. § 115.55, subds. 5, 5a (2002) expressly provided for local regulation. The owners failed to present a justiciable controversy on whether § 115.55, subds. 5, 5a, violated separation of powers by vesting quasi-judicial authority in licensed ISTS inspectors without a mechanism for judicial review. Section 115.55, subd. 5a did not violate due process by creating an irrebuttable presumption because it allowed for a meaningful opportunity to be heard and to dispute an inspector's finding of an imminent threat to public health or safety. Because the owners failed to demonstrate that Minn. R. 7080.0060, subtp. 3 (2003), which relied on the definitions in Minn. R. 7080.0020, subpts. 28e, 29a, 49b (2003) and the criteria in Minn. R. 7080.0110, subpt. 4 (2003), was indefinite or failed to provide adequate standards for enforcement, the rules were not void for vagueness.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Board's decision.

State v. Alger


A03-1701


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1129



September 28, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


PRIOR HISTORY: Winona County; Hon. Jeffrey Thompson, Judge. District Court No. T0-03-1304.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After his motion to suppress was denied, defendant was convicted of fourth-degree driving while intoxicated in the Winona County District Court, Minnesota. Defendant appealed, asserting the trial court erred in denying his motion, because the arresting officer could not have had a reasonable, articulable suspicion, that he had committed or was committing a traffic violation, so as to conduct an investigatory stop.


OVERVIEW: Two officers were patrolling in the vicinity where defendant was stopped. The first officer radioed to an officer ahead that he observed a full-sized pickup speeding. The second officer pulled over and observed a small pickup pass by. At about the time defendant came by, the first officer clarified that defendant's pickup was actually a mid-sized pickup, and he described it as to its model. The second officer then stopped defendant's pickup. Defendant argued that, because the snowstorm made visibility difficult and there were many pickup trucks in the subject town, there was not a reasonable objective basis for the stop. However, the appellate court noted the second officer observed that defendant's vehicle was travelling much faster than the smaller vehicle and there were no ingress or egress routes between the two points where either officer observed defendant's vehicle. Thus, additional pickup trucks entering onto the highway would have been unlikely. The appellate court held the second officer was entitled to act on the first officer's tip, and that, based on the totality of the circumstances, the second officer had a particularized and objective basis for making the stop.


OUTCOME: The trial court's denial of defendant's motion to suppress evidence, and his conviction, were affirmed.

State v. Bohanon


A03-1528


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1067



September 21, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


PRIOR HISTORY: Ramsey County; Hon. Paulette K. Flynn Judge.District Court File No. K7-02-3448.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After denying defendant's motion to suppress evidence of his resistive conduct, the Ramsey County District Court, Minnesota, convicted defendant of obstructing legal process with force. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant contended the trial court erred by denying his motion to suppress evidence of his resistive conduct because it was the result of an unconstitutional seizure. The appellate court disagreed, holding that, while there was conflicting testimony, the trial court heard both parties and found the officer's version of events to be more credible. The trial court did not, therefore, err by concluding that the officer had reasonable, articulable suspicion sufficient to stop defendant, and it did not err by denying his motion to suppress evidence resulting from the stop. Further, it was not error for the trial court to prevent the jury from playing the dispatch tape in the jury room; instead the jury was required to listen to the tape again only after reconvening in the courtroom with the parties present, which was the preferable method. The jury could have believed the officers' testimony rather than defendant's and found that there was aggression or provocation by defendant. Thus, there was sufficient evidence for a reasonable jury to conclude that defendant was not acting in self-defense.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

David E. McNally Dev. Corp. v. City of Winona


A03-1785


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


686 N.W.2d 553; 2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1071



September 21, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Winona County District Court. File No. CX-02-604.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged a judgment of the Winona County District Court (Minnesota), which set aside its assessment and which ordered a reassessment of all property covered by the assessment roll in respondent real estate developer's action contesting the assessment against it.


OVERVIEW: The developer argued that the assessment exceeded the benefit to the property and that the assessment was not uniform on the same class of property. The court held that the testimony of the developer's president failed to rebut the presumption of validity created by the city's approval of its assessment roll because he only testified in general terms. While the city was allowed discretion to determine categories of property benefited by improvements, under Winona, Minn., Ordinance 3481, it had to allocate the cost of the improvement to the gross developable acreage benefited. Accordingly, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in determining that the city failed to comply with the ordinance in making that allocation because the city did not determine the extent of the property that benefited from the construction of the smaller diameter trunk lines, as required by the ordinance. When considering a challenge to a city's assessment, the trial court had authority to order a reassessment of all the property on the roll if the challenge involved the validity of an entire assessment, which it had in the developer's case. Thus, the trial court properly ordered reassessment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's disapproval of the assessment roll and remanded the cause to the city for reassessment not inconsistent with the court's opinion.

Morris v. State


A04-683, A04-725


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1087



September 21, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Morris v. State, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 749 (2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Dakota County; Hon. Richard G. Spicer, Judge. District Court. File No. K4-01-1837.
State v. Morris, 2003 Minn. App. LEXIS 100 (Minn. Ct. App., Feb. 4, 2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Dakota County District Court (Minnesota), which denied his petitions for postconviction relief. Defendant had been convicted of four counts of harassment and one count of impersonating a police officer. The trial court found that he was a dangerous repeat offender under Minn. Stat. § 609.1095 (2000) and sentenced him to an executed, upward double departure.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that he was entitled to relief on various grounds, including a claim that he was denied effective assistance of counsel. In affirming, the court held that all of defendant's claims were either previously raised by him in his direct appeal or known to him at that time. Thus, they could not be considered in a subsequent postconviction relief proceeding. Defendant made no claim, and the record did not indicate, that the three enumerated exceptions to the general rule that matters raised or known to defendant at the time of direct appeal were barred from consideration of postconviction relief applied. Accordingly, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying defendant's petition that challenged his sentence without an evidentiary hearing. The court concluded that defendant did not meet his burden of proving ineffective assistance of appellate counsel. He failed to provide a credible argument as to why his attorney should have raised the claim that Minn. Stat. § 609.749 (2000), the statute under which he was convicted, was vague and overbroad and also failed to show that, but for the alleged errors, the results of his case would have been different.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

State v. Goodwin


A03-1807


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


686 N.W.2d 40; 2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 1013



September 7, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Goodwin, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 823 (Minn., Dec. 14, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Dakota County District Court. File No. K3-01-3885.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a search warrant was executed, defendant was arrested and charged with multiple violations of controlled-substance laws. In response to defendant's argument that the warrant was invalid, the Dakota County District Court, Minnesota, concluded that the unannounced entry was justified by the information contained in the supporting affidavit for the warrant and that application of the exclusionary rule was not appropriate. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant contended the trial court erred: in determining that reasonable suspicion existed to justify an unannounced entry into the apartment; and in determining that the exclusionary rule should not apply to evidence seized by police when the warrant was executed one minute and seven seconds before the permitted time frame. The appellate court disagreed, finding that a reasonable suspicion that the safety of the officers would have been threatened if the unannounced-entry provision was not part of the warrant based on information in the affidavit stating that the person who lived in the apartment was engaged in drug sales, was involved in a gang, and frequently carried a gun. The appellate court also concluded that the warrant, executed less than two minutes before the applicable time for the commencement of the search, did not constitute a constitutional violation. It was unlikely such a de minimis departure from the statutory requirement would have be so unexpected so as to cause a surprise intrusion into defendant's privacy beyond the invasion that would have occurred if the search was properly executed one minute and seven seconds later.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

501 of 1980 DOCUMENTS


State v. Griffin


A03-963


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 994



August 31, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Griffin, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 843 (2004)
Post-conviction relief denied at Griffin v. State, 2008 Minn. App. Unpub. LEXIS 793 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Hennepin Count; Hon. Katherian D. Roey, Judge. District Court File No. 02101945.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Hennepin County District Court (Minnesota), which, in a jury trial, convicted defendant of first-degree criminal sexual conduct and kidnapping.


OVERVIEW: Defendant abducted the victim from a bus stop and sexually assaulted her. The victim, a diabetic, had been on her way to a medical facility for treatment at the time of the offense. The court found no error in the district court's admission, under Minn. R. Evid. 609(a)(1), of defendant's prior conviction for first-degree assault. Although somewhat remote, the prior conviction demonstrated significant disregard for the law and thus was probative of truthfulness. Because of the victim's medical condition, describing her as a vulnerable adult was not prosecutorial misconduct. The prosecutor drew reasonable inferences from the DNA evidence introduced at trial. The prosecutor improperly appealed to prejudice by inviting the jury to consider the crime's impact on the community and by eliciting sympathy for the victim, but those errors were harmless. Although the district court had permissive authority under Minn. Sentencing Guidelines II.F to impose consecutive sentences and did so based on a finding of particular cruelty under Minn. Sentencing Guidelines II.D.2.b(2), the court remanded, because the district court found aggravating factors under a preponderance-of-the-evidence standard.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and remanded for consideration of whether the upward departure was unconstitutional in light of recent federal case law.

 

502 of 1980 DOCUMENTS


State v. Davis


A03-1491


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 998



August 31, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Davis, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 729 (Minn., Nov. 16, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Ramsey County; Hon. Gregg E. Johnson, Judge. District Court File No. K103734.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Ramsey County District Court (Minnesota), which, in a jury trial, convicted defendant of assault and ineligible possession of a firearm.


OVERVIEW: The victim was shot during a robbery attempt. The victim selected defendant's photo from a photographic lineup and said that he was 85 percent sure that defendant was the shooter. Police then showed the victim a profile photograph of defendant, and the victim identified defendant as the shooter. Because the victim selected defendant from the lineup with a significant degree of certainty, the court concluded that showing the second photograph was not impermissibly suggestive. Although police informed the victim about defendant's confession after the victim identified him as the shooter, the in-court identification was not tainted. Defendant's due process rights were not violated, because the identification procedures did not cause a substantial likelihood of irreparable misidentification. Defendant was not entitled to a mistrial regarding the admission of evidence about a prior conviction, because defendant did not stipulate that he was ineligible to possess a firearm. Moreover, any instructional error was harmless, as the district court gave a curative instruction. Counsel's failure to stipulate that defendant was ineligible to possess a firearm was not ineffective assistance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction.

 

503 of 1980 DOCUMENTS


State v. Lenear


A03-1309


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 968



August 24, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Lenear, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 726 (2004)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by State v. Lenear, 2007 Minn. App. Unpub. LEXIS 494 (2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Hennepin County; Hon. Delila F. Pierce, Judge. District Court File No. 02081050.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and remanded in part with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Hennepin County District Court, Minnesota, for first-degree criminal sexual conduct and sentenced accordingly. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the extension of the statute of limitations after his offenses were committed was an unconstitutional ex post facto law. He further contended that the upward durational sentence imposed by the trial court was error. The appellate court noted that because the statute of limitations had not yet expired on defendant's 1993 offense, an amendment extended the statute of limitations on that offense to nine years; therefore, under the new amendment, defendant had to be charged with the offense no later than November 11, 2002. The appellate court found that because defendant was charged on October 22, 2002, the statute of limitations had not yet expired, and because the 1995 extension of the statute of limitations occurred before the 1993 statute of limitations had expired, it survived his ex post facto challenge. However, the appellate court held that an upward departure under Washington's determinate sentencing scheme was found by the United States Supreme Court to have violated a defendant's Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial; thus, the trial court had to consider that precedent in resentencing defendant.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed in part, but reversed and remanded in part for resentencing.

 

504 of 1980 DOCUMENTS


Council of Indep. Tobacco Mfrs. of Am. v. State


A03 -2020


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


685 N.W.2d 467; 2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 983



August 24, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by Council of Indep. Tobacco Mfrs. of Am. v. State, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 739 (2004)
Affirmed by Council of Indep. Tobacco Mfrs. of Am. v. State, 713 N.W.2d 300, 2006 Minn. LEXIS 118 (Minn., 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Ramsey County District Court. File No. C1037120.
State by Humphrey v. Philip Morris, 1995 Minn. LEXIS 784 (Minn., Sept. 20, 1995)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, manufacturers and distributors of cigarettes who were not parties to State v. Philip Morris, Inc., which ended in a settlement agreement, brought an action against appellee State to prohibit enforcement of Minn. Stat. § 297F.24 (Supp. 2003), alleging that the statute violated, inter alia, the First Amendment. The Ramsey County District Court (Minnesota) granted summary judgment to the State. The manufacturers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The first issue was whether Minn. Stat. § 297F.24 (Supp. 2003) abridged the manufacturers' First Amendment right of free speech. The purpose of § 297F.24, was to require non-settlement manufacturers to pay fees comparable to the costs incurred by the State attributable to the use of cigarettes and to prevent non-settlement manufacturers from flooding the State with cheap cigarettes, thereby undermining the State's policy of discouraging youth smokers. The Court of Appeals of Minnesota held that was a legitimate purpose within the State's power and, because it was not a direct attempt to regulate speech, did not offend the First Amendment. The classifications in the statute between settling manufacturers, who paid annual fees that subsidized the costs to the state of smoking and acted to raise cigarette prices to discourage youthful smokers, and non-settling manufacturers, who did not, created a genuine and substantial distinction tied to a legitimate state interest. As such, Minn. Stat. § 297F.24 was not special legislation prohibited by the Minnesota Constitution. Section 297F.24 (Supp. 2003) was not a direct attempt to regulate speech but had a legitimate legislative purpose.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Koch v. Sheldahl


A03-1562


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 980



August 19, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


PRIOR HISTORY: Department of Employment and Economic Development. File No. 10403 03.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Minnesota Department of Employment and Economic Development reversed an unemployment law judge's decision granting relator claimant unemployment compensation benefits on the ground that she was discharged for aggravated employment misconduct and held that she was required to repay benefits she received. The claimant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The claimant cashed three pay checks from her employer when she knew she was only entitled to receive two paychecks. Her employer terminated her employment for attempted theft of company funds. On appeal, the claimant argued that her conduct did not constitute aggravated employment misconduct, the statutory provisions requiring a claimant who was ultimately determined to be disqualified to repay benefits were unconstitutional, and that the notice she received regarding her obligation to repay benefits was defective and violated her due-process rights. The appellate court held that the record reasonably supported the department's finding that the claimant's acts would have amounted to the crime of theft and that, therefore, she committed aggravated employment misconduct. The appellate court further held that the claimant failed to explain how the repayment procedure violated constitutional due-process rights. The appellate court concluded that the unemployment law judge's decision was not required to provide the claimant with notice of the consequences of losing an appeal.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the department was affirmed.

State v. Rivera


A03-1596


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 962



August 17, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


PRIOR HISTORY: Hennepin County; Hon. Mark S. Wernick, Judge. District Court File No. 2095647.



DISPOSITION: Conviction affirmed; remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Hennepin County Court, Minnesota, convicting him of second-degree assault, Minn. Stat. § 609.222, subd. 1 (2002), and sentencing him to 42 months in prison.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and the complainant began dating, and defendant moved into the complainant's residence. Defendant did not deny choking the complainant with a chain. Defendant said he wanted to talk to the complainant that morning, he was "ashamed" that the complainant wanted him to leave, and then his "whole body felt like it left." During this out-of-body experience, defendant choked the complainant with a chain. The appellate court ruled that no rational basis existed for the jury to convict defendant of fifth-degree assault, a lesser-included offense of second-degree assault, and acquit him of second-degree assault. Defendant choked the complainant with a chain, chain marks remained visible on the complainant's neck for several days after the assault, and she temporarily lost her voice. The chain was clearly used in a manner calculated to cause bodily harm. However, the case would have to be remanded for resentencing, because the district court imposed a double durational departure on its own findings that the victim was particularly vulnerable, the offense was done in a particularly cruel way, and the crime was committed in the presence of children.


OUTCOME: The conviction was affirmed, and the case was remanded for resentencing.

 

507 of 1980 DOCUMENTS


State v. Modtland


A04-17


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 918



August 10, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by State v. Modtland, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 656 (2004)
Reversed by, Remanded by State v. Modtland, 695 N.W.2d 602, 2005 Minn. LEXIS 269 (Minn., May 12, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Kanabec County; Hon. Krista K. Martin, Judge. District Court File No. K5-03-101.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to charges of first- and second-degree burglary in the Kanabec County District Court, Minnesota. Per a plea agreement, the trial court stayed execution of defendant's sentence and granted his request for probation with the requirement that he complete a drug-and-alcohol treatment program. Defendant failed to complete the program, and the trial court later revoked his probation and imposed his sentence. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The record established that defendant was discharged from the treatment program because of hostile conduct directed at staff members, including repeated threats to sue the sponsors of the treatment program. The district court was required to address three factors when revoking a defendant's probation. The district court did not explicitly address the three factors. However, because defendant did not assert at the probation-revocation hearing that said factors had not been satisfied, the appellate court could affirm the revocation of his probation if there was sufficient record evidence to show that those factors were met. Only the third factor was at issue. That was, that the need for confinement outweighed the policies favoring probation. Defendant's probation officer had recommended that his sentence be imposed, because he "seemed to be a huge risk to public safety." Defendant himself impliedly admitted that his behavior in the treatment program was improper. Further, the record also showed that the seriousness of his probation violation would have been unduly depreciated if his probation had not been revoked. Thus, the trial court properly revoked defendant's probation.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court, revoking defendant's probation and executing his sentence, was affirmed.

 

508 of 1980 DOCUMENTS


In re Universal Underwriters Life Ins. Co.


A04-184


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


685 N.W.2d 44; 2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 920



August 10, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Commissioner of Commerce Agency. File No. 1-1004-14813-2.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, the Minnesota Commissioner of Commerce, notified relator life insurance company (the company) that it intended to disallow the company's credit insurance rates. The company moved for summary disposition. Following a hearing, an administrative law judge (ALJ) denied the company's motion and recommended disallowing the rates. The recommendation was adopted, and the company sought review.


OVERVIEW: It was undisputed that the rates were within the prima facie range established by law and were therefore presumptively reasonable. However, under Minn. Stat. 62B.07, subd. 2 (2002), even when rates met the latter standard, the Commissioner had to disapprove an insurance form if the premium rates were excessive in relation to benefits. The company paid out in claims only 20 percent of the amount collected for credit life insurance and 18 percent of the amount collected for credit disability insurance. An actuary testified that the company's extremely high commission rates were excessive in comparison to commissions that were paid in other states for similar policies. Specifically, the company paid 53 percent of the credit life insurance premiums and 50 percent of the credit disability premiums for commissions to agents. Consequently, the appellate court found the agency's conclusions were not arbitrary and capricious. The appellate court found the Commissioner successfully rebutted the presumption of reasonableness of the company's rates and rejected the company's argument that the Commissioner engaged in unpromulgated rulemaking by its disapproval.


OUTCOME: The decision of the lower tribunal was affirmed.

State v. Smith


A03-1157


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 882



August 3, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Minn. v. Thaddeus Smith, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 684 (Minn., Oct. 19, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: St. Louis County; Hon. Mark A. Munger, Judge. District Court File No. K602600393.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the St. Louis County District Court (Minnesota) of second-degree intentional murder and attempted second-degree intentional murder. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by denying his motion for a continuance. The court of appeals disagreed. The trial court denied the motion for a continuance because it was untimely, and defendant failed to demonstrate that any prejudice would result from the denial. Defendant failed to offer any specific reason why he was dissatisfied with his attorneys. He said only that he "didn't like the way that my case was being represented at this point in time, and I do not feel comfortable with the situation." Defendant argued that his uneasiness was delayed because he did not have extensive contact with his attorneys until just prior to trial. But there was nothing in the record to support that claim or to explain what made defendant "uneasy" other than his statement that he did not believe that his attorneys were representing him to the best of their ability. Because defendant failed to show that he was prejudiced by his attorneys' representation, and the motion for continuance was untimely, the trial court did not abuse its discretion by denying defendant's motion for a continuance to obtain private counsel.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Crawford


A03-1201


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 902



August 3, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Minn. v. Wiley Lee Crawford, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 677 (Minn., Oct. 19, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Ramsey County; Hon. A. James Dickinson, Judge. District Court File No. KX-03-456.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Ramsey County District Court (Minnesota), which convicted him of simple robbery.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court found that, considering the totality of the circumstances, defendant's arrest was clearly justified. Contrary to defendant's assertions, the victim's shopping bag and handbag were not innocuous when they matched the description of the stolen property, were recovered early on a holiday morning from a vehicle that matched the description of the vehicle in which the suspect fled, and were found between the legs of a man matching the description of the suspect. The court further found no merit to defendant's contention that all evidence relating to the show-up identification should have been suppressed. The victim's consistent testimony both before and after the show-up indicated that the procedure was not unnecessarily suggestive. Though the victim was unable to view the perpetrator's face, she was able to ascertain his race, gender, stature, hand size, and clothing at the time of the crime. Finally, the court found that the evidence was sufficient to prove beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant was the perpetrator of the crime. His clothing, race, and gender matched the description of the perpetrator.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.

 

511 of 1980 DOCUMENTS


Rukavina v. Pawlenty


A03-1709


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


684 N.W.2d 525; 2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 898



July 28, 2004, Decided  
August 3, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Tom Rukavina v. Tim Pawlenty, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 674 (Minn., Oct. 19, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Ramsey County District Court. File No. C1032239.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part; motion to supplement record denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, three taxpayers, two legislators, and an association, challenged a decision from the Ramsey County District Court (Minnesota), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondents, a governor and a finance commissioner, in a case involving a challenge to the constitutionality of Minn. Stat. § 16A.152, subd. 4 (2002).


OVERVIEW: The finance commissioner reduced a mineral fund in response to a state deficit under the provisions of Minn. Stat. § 16A.152, subd. 4 (2002). Thereafter, the taxpayers, the legislators, and the association challenged the action. The district court granted summary judgment after finding that there was no standing to proceed with the action. On review, the court determined that the taxpayers lacked standing because the challenges were primarily based on their disagreement with the policy or exercise of discretion by those responsible for executing the law. Further, the legislators did not have standing because they did not show any injury that was other than an institutional one. However, the association had standing because of its mission to promote economic development in mining regions. However, summary judgment was properly granted because the finance commissioner had authority under § 16A.152, subd. 4 to reduce the mineral fund to meet the budget shortfall. Money in the mineral fund at the time of the reduction was not "expended" within the meaning of § 16A.152, subd. 4(b). Finally, § 16A.152 did not unconstitutionally violate the separation-of-powers doctrine.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the district court's decision regarding the fact that the association lacked standing to pursue the case. However, the decision to grant summary judgment on the merits was affirmed. A motion to supplement the record was denied.

Hageman v. Stanek


A03-2045


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 789



July 13, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Hagerman v. Stanek, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 607 (Minn., Sept. 21, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Hennepin County; Hon. John Q. McShane, Judge. District Court. File No. MC 03-9557.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizens sought review of the judgment of the Hennepin County District Court (Minnesota), which dismissed their action against respondents, the Minnesota Commissioner of Public Safety and a domestic abuse agency, to challenge the constitutionality of Minn. Stat. §§ 611A.31-611A.375 (2002) on equal protection grounds and alleged gender discrimination.


OVERVIEW: Sections 611A.31-611A.375 established grants of appropriated funds to local programs to provide services for victims of domestic abuse. The citizens argued that §§ 611A.31-611A.375 were unconstitutional, as they provided no funding or services for male victims of domestic abuse and discriminated against men. The citizens claimed that they had standing based upon their status as residents and taxpayers of Minnesota. None of the citizens had sought shelter or services due to domestic violence or had been denied those services based on their gender. On appeal, the court held that the action was properly dismissed for lack of standing. The court ruled that to have standing to seek a declaratory judgment regarding the constitutionality of a state statute, the citizens were required to have a direct interest in the validity of that statute. Further, the citizens were required to have sustained or have been in immediate danger of sustaining a direct injury from the enforcement of the challenged statute and not in an indefinite way in common with the general public. The citizens failed to show that they had been injured or that their interest was different than that of other citizens.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

In re Welfare of K. J. E.


A03 -1652


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 811



July 13, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by In re The Welfare Of: K.J.E., 2004 Minn. LEXIS 662 (Minn., Oct. 19, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Hennepin County; Hon. Lloyd B. Zimmerman, Judge. District Court File Nos. 185043, J2-03-60324.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Hennepin County District Court (Minnesota), which adjudicated him delinquent on two counts of controlled substance crime.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant challenged the denial of his pretrial motion to suppress the drug evidence on the ground that the stop by police officers was unconstitutional. The court found that, upon first spotting the officers, defendant began walking away from them. He reversed his direction once the officers were out of sight and moved in the same direction they had gone, only to reverse his course again and move more quickly once he saw that the officers were returning. The fact that defendant's behavior could reasonably be construed by the officers as an attempt to evade them gave the officers reasonable suspicion to stop defendant without more. The court further found that defendant was involved in a disturbance in a high-crime area. More importantly, defendant, who appeared to the officers to be in his late teens, appeared to be carrying a concealed weapon, in violation of Minn. Stat. § 624.714, subd. 2(b)(2) (Supp. 2003). Accordingly, the officers had a reasonable, articulable suspicion of criminal activity to stop defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court.

State v. Hanson


A03-1020


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 820



July 13, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Hanson, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 680 (Minn., Oct. 19, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Hennepin County; Hon. Francis J. Connolly, Judge. District Court File No. 02026468.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Hennepin County District Court (Minnesota) of three counts of criminal sexual conduct and one count of indecent exposure. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the district court erred by denying his motion to suppress evidence because there was no probable cause for the search warrant. The court of appeals disagreed. The affidavit supporting the warrant application outlined the allegations of the victims, identified defendant as having been investigated for child molestation in 1998, and it stated that when the police arrived to arrest defendant, he was in the presence of a nine-year-old boy whom he had befriended while driving the school bus. Defendant and the boy were looking at a computer, and police found two photographs of juvenile males in defendant's wallet when he was arrested. The warrant affidavit also outlined the officers' combined training and experience of more than 15 years in sex crimes investigations. Therefore, the search warrant was supported by probable cause.


OUTCOME: The indecent exposure sentence was vacated and the conditional release term was modified. The judgment was otherwise affirmed.

Burch v. State


A03-1750


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 814



July 7, 2004, Decided  
July 13, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Burch v. State, 2004 Minn. LEXIS 630 (2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Hennepin County; Hon. Stephen D. Swanson, Judge. District Court File No. 96035800.
Burch v. State, 2003 Minn. App. LEXIS 468 (Minn. Ct. App., Apr. 22, 2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In 1996, defendant was convicted of first-degree burglary. Defendant brought a second petition for postconviction relief challenging his conviction. The Hennepin County District Court, Minnesota, denied his petition. Defendant appealed, challenging whether he was mentally competent to assist in his defense and whether the warrantless search of his car was lawful. Defendant also asserted several other errors as to his trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant raised claims that could have been raised in his first postconviction proceeding, and those claims were waived. However, the appellate court chose to address certain claims in the interests of justice. Defendant argued that the prosecutor committed misconduct by commenting at the Rasmussen hearing on his decision to exercise his Fifth Amendment privilege and not answer a question about his prior knowledge of stolen property in the trunk of his vehicle. The prosecutor had argued that the trial court should have drawn adverse conclusions from defendant's invocation of his Fifth Amendment right. However, the next day, the prosecutor corrected the error on the record and made sure that defendant understood that his testimony during the Rasmussen hearing could not be used against him in the case in chief other than for impeachment purposes. The appellate court held that, as the comment was made to the trial court, not a jury, there was no prosecutorial misconduct. Finally, the appellate court rejected defendant's argument that he was improperly sentenced as a career-offender. Defendant had five prior felony convictions, which sufficed for his sentence as a career offender.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Livingston v. Minn. Racing Comm'n


A03-1605


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2004 Minn. App. LEXIS 719



June 29, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


PRIOR HISTORY: Minnesota Racing Commission. File No. MRC # 708910.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relators, a racehorse owner and his trainer, appealed an order of respondent, Minnesota Racing Commission, which affirmed the board of stewards' 30-day suspension of the trainer because of a positive finding of albuterol in a urine sample taken from a horse he trained after the horse won a race, which affirmed the disqualification of the horse, and which required the owner to return and redistribute the purse money.


OVERVIEW: Relators argued that the commission's ruling violated their due-process rights and that the rules on which the suspension and redistribution order were based were invalid. In affirming, the court found that the trainer had proper notice of the proceedings before the board, as he had been notified via telephone and had been present at the hearing via telephone. Under Minn. R. 7877.0170, subpt. 2J, because the trainer had notice of the proceedings, the owner was also deemed to have notice of the proceedings. Moreover, the owner was sent a notice of the hearing. Accordingly, relators were not deprived of due process of law. Relators failed to show that Minn. R. 7877.0170, subpt. 2C, was unconstitutional, as ensuring the integrity of horse racing in Minnesota was a legitimate public purpose, and the rule was rationally related to achieving that public purpose. The commission's decision was justified, because the fact that the horse tested positive for albuterol was prima facie evidence that the trainer was responsible for the drug's presence in the horse's system under Minn. R. 7890.0130 (2003). The return of the purse money was not a fine or penalty under the Commission's rules.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Commission's order.
