597 of 1980 DOCUMENTS


In re S.D.N.


C3-02-1958


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2003 Minn. App. LEXIS 577



May 13, 2003, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by In re Welfare of S.D.N., 2003 Minn. LEXIS 492 (Minn., Aug. 5, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Ramsey County District Court. File No. J902553864. Hon. Michael F. Fetsch.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with one count of possession of a pistol by a minor, in violation of Minn. Stat. § 624.713, subds. 1(a), 2 (2002). At the Rasmussen hearing, defendant challenged the validity of a pat-down search and sought suppression of the pistol. The Ramsey County District Court (Minnesota) determined that none of the Curtis exceptions applied and suppressed the pistol as the fruit of an illegal pat-down search. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The district court concluded that the pat-down search was illegal, as defendant was not under arrest and because he was not exhibiting conduct that would indicate that he might have been a threat to the officers. Although the city curfew ordinance had no specific stated purpose, the appellate court assumed that its intent was to give police officers the authority to remove juveniles from environments where they could be in danger of becoming victims of crime or could themselves participate in illegal conduct. The officers observed a group of individuals, who looked like juveniles and were wearing gang colors, standing at a bus shelter after curfew and throwing garbage onto the street. Defendant was with a known and violent gang member. Those observations gave the officers a reasonable articulable suspicion to believe that there was a curfew violation. The moment the officers were required to transport defendant in the squad car, the pat-down search was necessary for the officers' protection. It was also reasonable for the officers to assume that an adult was not supervising defendant. Their failure to make further inquiries about an adult supervisor was reasonable.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed, and the case was remanded.

Allen v. City of Minneapolis


C1-02-1506


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2003 Minn. App. LEXIS 505



April 23, 2003, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Allen v. City of Minneapolis (In re Allen), 2003 Minn. LEXIS 425 (Minn., July 15, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Hennepin County District Court. File No. AP013979.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a special-assessment appeal, appellant property owners, whose property was assessed to pay for a street-lighting project completed by appellee city, moved to certify a class action, but their motion was denied by the Hennepin County District Court (Minnesota). The city moved for summary judgment, which the district court granted. The property owners appealed.


OVERVIEW: At the public hearing, city transportation engineer stated that the project was the result of a neighborhood petition that circulated for three years. The city engineer also stated that the petition met the city's threshold requirement of approval by 65 percent of the affected property owners. The city council adopted a resolution directing the installation of decorative street lighting and the use of special assessments to pay the cost of the project. The property owners who objected to the special assessment appealed to the district court, which granted the city's motion for summary judgment. The appellate court determined that the property owners' affidavits did not create a fact issue for district, because, when viewed in the light most favorable to the property owners, the affidavits did not establish the property owners' claim that the decorative lighting installed in the neighborhood did not increase the market value of any property in the neighborhood. Additionally, the appellate court found that a statutory remedy was available and adequate; thus, the district court did not err in denying the property owners' motion to certify a class action.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the district court's judgment.

 

599 of 1980 DOCUMENTS


State v. Dubiel


CX-02-905


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2003 Minn. App. LEXIS 388



April 2, 2003, Decided  
April 8, 2003, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


PRIOR HISTORY: Hennepin County District Court. File No. 01019117. Hon. John J. Sommerville.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's motion to suppress the evidence seized on the basis that the warrant application did not contain particularized reasonable suspicion to justify a nighttime search was denied and, following a court trial, defendant was convicted of and sentenced in the Hennepin County District Court, Minnesota, for fifth-degree controlled-substance crime. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The district court found that the warrant application did not contain sufficiently particularized reason to justify a nighttime or no-knock entry. However, the district court further found that the violation of Minn. Stat. § 626.14 (2000) was technical in nature and denied defendant's motion to suppress. On appeal defendant argued, inter alia, that the admittedly defective nighttime execution provision of the warrant was a constitutional violation requiring suppression of the evidence seized. The appellate court agree. Although police witnessed defendant clothed and sitting in his living room before executing the search warrant, and knocked and announced themselves, the warrant was executed almost two hours later than the statute allowed, there were no people coming or going from the residence, and the door was closed. Coupled with lack of any articulated suspicion to justify a nighttime search, these facts made the search unlawful and required suppression of the evidence. To hold otherwise, the court reasoned, would eliminate any requirement that a warrant meet the statutory requirements so long as officers see a clothed, awake person in a dwelling to be searched.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed defendant's conviction on the grounds that unlawful nighttime execution of the search warrant required suppression of the evidence seized and that the district court, therefore, erred by failing to suppress the evidence.

 

600 of 1980 DOCUMENTS


State v. Pisansky


C1-02-873


COURT OF APPEALS OF MINNESOTA


2003 Minn. App. LEXIS 356



April 1, 2003, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] THIS OPINION WILL BE UNPUBLISHED AND MAY NOT BE CITED EXCEPT AS PROVIDED BY MINNESOTA STATUTES.


PRIOR HISTORY: Ramsey County District Court. File No. T802602505. Hon. A. James Dickinson.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Ramsey County District Court, Minnesota, convicting him of violating New Brighton, Minn., City Ordinance § 9-41, prohibiting parking in a designated fire lane.


OVERVIEW: Defendant parked his vehicle near his condominium complex, while he retrieved some items from his home. Defendant parked the vehicle in the western condominium parking lot, to the east of an area that was marked in yellow as a fire lane. Defendant's vehicle was on a concrete sidewalk near the condominium garages. No part of defendant's vehicle was in the area designated as a fire lane. Defendant was ticketed for violating New Brighton, Minn., City Ordinance § 9-41. Defendant argued that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague, and that the evidence admitted at trial was insufficient to support his conviction. The appellate court ruled that the ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague, however, evidence that defendant blocked access to a fire lane was insufficient to support his conviction. A literal reading of the ordinance, and New Brighton, Minn., City Resolution No. 87-107 (adopted Oct. 27, 1987), prohibited only parking in designated fire lanes. No applicable ordinance or statute precluded defendant from parking on the sidewalk adjacent to a designated fire lane.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.
